<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Meditation">Meditation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_health&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Meditation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">Meditation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness-based_cognitive_therapy" title="Mindfulness-based cognitive therapy">Mindfulness-based cognitive therapy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meditation.flickr.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Meditation.flickr.jpg/220px-Meditation.flickr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Meditation.flickr.jpg/330px-Meditation.flickr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Meditation.flickr.jpg/440px-Meditation.flickr.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meditation.flickr.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woman towards the right and a man towards the left. Both sitting cross legged with their eyes closed. Their hands resting on their knees with their palms facing up.</div></div></div>
<p>The practice of mindfulness meditation has several potential mental health benefits, such as bringing about reductions in <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_stress" title="Psychological stress">stress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goyal_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goyal-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galla_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galla-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharma_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khoury_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khoury-119">[119]</a></sup> Mindfulness <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a> may also be effective in treating substance use disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chiesa_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chiesa-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lucid_Dreaming">Lucid Dreaming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_health&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Lucid Dreaming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucid_dreaming" title="Lucid dreaming">Lucid dreaming</a> has been found to be associated with greater mental well-being. It also was not associated with poorer sleep quality nor with cognitive dissociation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> There is also some evidence lucid dreaming therapy can help with nightmare reduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mental_fitness">Mental fitness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_health&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Mental fitness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Mental fitness is a mental health movement that encourages people to intentionally regulate and maintain their emotional wellbeing through friendship, regular human contact, and activities that include meditation, calming exercises, aerobic exercise, mindfulness, having a routine and maintaining adequate sleep. Mental fitness is intended to build resilience against every-day mental health challenges to prevent an escalation of anxiety, depression and suicidal ideation, and help them cope with the escalation of those feelings if they occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiritual_counseling">Spiritual counseling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_health&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Spiritual counseling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Spiritual counsellors meet with people in need to offer comfort and support and to help them gain a better understanding of their issues and develop a problem-solving relation with <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a>. These types of counselors deliver care based on spiritual, psychological and theological principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_and_public_health_policies">Laws and public health policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_health&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Laws and public health policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mental_health_law" title="Mental health law">Mental health law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">Public health</a></div>
<p>There are many factors that influence mental health including:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">Mental illness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disability</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> are ultimately the result of a combination of biology, environment, and access to and utilization of mental health treatment.</li>
<li>Public health policies can influence access and utilization, which subsequently may improve mental health and help to progress the negative consequences of depression and its associated disability.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_health&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Emotional mental illnesses should be a particular concern in the United States since the U.S. has the highest annual prevalence rates (26 percent) for mental illnesses among a comparison of 14 developing and developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> While approximately 80 percent of all people in the United States with a mental disorder eventually receive some form of treatment, on average persons do not access care until nearly a decade following the development of their illness, and less than one-third of people who seek help receive minimally adequate care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The government offers everyone programs and services, but veterans receive the most help, there is certain eligibility criteria that has to be met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Policies"><span class="anchor" id="Mental_health_policies_in_the_United_States"></span>Policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mental_health&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Mental health policies in the United States have experienced four major reforms: the American asylum movement led by <a href="/wiki/Dorothea_Dix" title="Dorothea Dix">Dorothea Dix</a> in 1843; the mental hygiene movement inspired by <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Whittingham_Beers" title="Clifford Whittingham Beers">Clifford Beers</a> in 1908; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deinstitutionalization" title="Deinstitutionalization">deinstitutionalization</a> started by Action for Mental Health in 1961; and the community support movement called for by The CMCH Act Amendments of 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Public_Health_Service_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Public_Health_Service-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1843, <a href="/wiki/Dorothea_Dix" title="Dorothea Dix">Dorothea Dix</a> submitted a Memorial to the Legislature of Massachusetts, describing the abusive treatment and horrible conditions received by the mentally ill patients in jails, cages, and almshouses. She revealed in her Memorial: "I proceed, gentlemen, briefly to call your attention to the present state of insane persons confined within this Commonwealth, in cages, closets, cellars, stalls, pens! Chained, naked, beaten with rods, and lashed into obedience...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Many asylums were built in that period, with high fences or walls separating the patients from other community members and strict rules regarding the entrance and exit. In 1866, a recommendation came to the New York State Legislature to establish a separate asylum for chronic mentally ill patients. Some hospitals placed the chronic patients into separate wings or wards, or different buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>A Mind That Found Itself</i> (1908) <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Whittingham_Beers" title="Clifford Whittingham Beers">Clifford Whittingham Beers</a> described the humiliating treatment he received and the deplorable conditions in the mental hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> One year later, the National Committee for Mental Hygiene (NCMH) was founded by a small group of reform-minded scholars and scientists—including Beers himself—which marked the beginning of the "mental hygiene" movement. The movement emphasized the importance of childhood prevention. <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> catalyzed this idea with an additional emphasis on the impact of maladjustment, which convinced the hygienists that prevention was the only practical approach to handle mental health issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> However, prevention was not successful, especially for chronic illness; the condemnable conditions in the hospitals were even more prevalent, especially under the pressure of the increasing number of chronically ill and the influence of the depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Public_Health_Service_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Public_Health_Service-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1961, the Joint Commission on Mental Health published a report called Action for Mental Health, whose goal was for community clinic care to take on the burden of prevention and early intervention of the mental illness, therefore to leave space in the hospitals for severe and chronic patients. The court started to rule in favor of the patients' will on whether they should be forced to treatment. By 1977, 650 community mental health centers were built to cover 43 percent of the population and serve 1.9 million individuals a year, and the lengths of treatment decreased from 6 months to only 23 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koyanagi,_C._1991_p.899-905_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koyanagi,_C._1991_p.899-905-133">[133]</a></sup> However, issues still existed. Due to inflation, especially in the 1970s, the community nursing homes received less money to support the care and treatment provided. Fewer than half of the planned centers were created, and new methods did not fully replace the old approaches to carry out its full capacity of treating power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koyanagi,_C._1991_p.899-905_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koyanagi,_C._1991_p.899-905-133">[133]</a></sup> Besides, the community helping system was not fully established to support the patients' housing, vocational opportunities, income supports, and other benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Public_Health_Service_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Public_Health_Service-128">[128]</a></sup> Many patients returned to <a href="/wiki/Welfare" title="Welfare">welfare</a> and criminal justice institutions, and more became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeless" title="Homeless">homeless</a>. The movement of deinstitutionalization was facing great challenges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torrey_Book_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Torrey_Book-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>After realizing that simply changing the location of mental health care from the state hospitals to nursing houses was insufficient to implement the idea of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deinstitutionalization" title="Deinstitutionalization">deinstitutionalization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Mental_Health" title="National Institute of Mental Health">National Institute of Mental Health</a> (NIMH) in 1975 created the Community Support Program (CSP) to provide funds for communities to set up a comprehensive mental health service and supports to help the mentally ill patients integrate successfully in the society. The program stressed the importance of other supports in addition to medical care, including housing, living expenses, employment, transportation, and education; and set up new national priority for people with serious mental disorders. In addition, the Congress enacted the <a href="/wiki/Mental_Health_Systems_Act_of_1980" title="Mental Health Systems Act of 1980">Mental Health Systems Act of 1980</a> to prioritize the service to the mentally ill and emphasize the expansion of services beyond just clinical care alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koyanagi,_C._2007_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koyanagi,_C._2007-135">[135]</a></sup> Later in the 1980s, under the influence from the Congress and the Supreme Court, many programs started to help the patients regain their benefits. A new Medicaid service was also established to serve people who were diagnosed with a "chronic mental illness". People who were temporally hospitalized were also provided aid and care and a pre-release program was created to enable people to apply for reinstatement prior to discharge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koyanagi,_C._1991_p.899-905_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-Koyanagi,_C._1991_p.899-905-133">[133]</a></sup> Not until 1990, around 35 years after the start of the deinstitutionalization, did the first state hospital begin to close. The number of hospitals dropped from around 300 by over 40 in the 1990s, and finally a Report on Mental Health showed the efficacy of mental health treatment, giving a range of treatments available for patients to choose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koyanagi,_C._2007_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koyanagi,_C._2007-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, several critics maintain that deinstitutionalization has, from a mental health point of view, been a thoroughgoing failure. The seriously mentally ill are either homeless, or in prison; in either case (especially the latter), they are getting little or no mental health care. This failure is attributed to a number of reasons over which there is some degree of contention, although there is general agreement that community support programs have been ineffective at best, due to a lack of funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torrey_Book_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Torrey_Book-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2011 National Prevention Strategy included mental and emotional well-being, with recommendations including better parenting and early intervention programs, which increase the likelihood of prevention programs being included in future US mental health policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The NIMH is researching only suicide and <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> prevention, but the National Prevention Strategy could lead to it focusing more broadly on longitudinal prevention studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Source contains a list of publications. Suicide prevention appears only in sidebar links; HIV/AIDS is not mentioned. (January 2016)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 2013, United States Representative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timothy_F._Murphy" title="Timothy F. Murphy">Tim Murphy</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/National_Helping_Families_in_Mental_Health_Crisis_Day" title="National Helping Families in Mental Health Crisis Day">Helping Families in Mental Health Crisis Act, HR2646</a>. The bipartisan bill went through substantial revision and was reintroduced in 2015 by Murphy and Congresswoman <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Bernice_Johnson" title="Eddie Bernice Johnson">Eddie Bernice Johnson</a>. In November 2015, it passed the Health Subcommittee by an 18–12 vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
